<div id="review_257936300" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/257936300" />
    <a title="Jeff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/501971-jeff"><img alt="Jeff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592065078p2/501971.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/257936300?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeff" href="/user/show/501971-jeff">Jeff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781586380212" href="/book/show/1578131.The_Upanishads">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/501971-jeff?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/501971-jeff?shelf=holy_shit">holy_shit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer257936300" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6610928134354109279">Book #3 in 2012's survey of holy shit (#2 was <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/27317.Confucian_Analects__The_Great_Learning___The_Doctrine_of_the_Mean' title='Confucian Analects, The Great Learning & The Doctrine of the Mean by Confucius' rel='nofollow noopener'>Confucius's Analects etc.</a>).<br/><br/>Overall this collection of disparate mystical writings by long-dead Hindus is the early frontrunner for my Most Philosophically Stimulating Sacred Text award. I almost wrote <i>Theo</i>sophically, but their ideas exercised my love of wisdom more than they conveyed to me any wisdom about god. (Screw you, too, Blavatsky! i know you're listening.)<br/><br/>I disagreed with most of the metaphysical claims. I quibbled frequently. I pooh-poohed a</span>
  <span id="freeText6610928134354109279" style="display:none">Book #3 in 2012's survey of holy shit (#2 was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/27317.Confucian_Analects__The_Great_Learning___The_Doctrine_of_the_Mean" title="Confucian Analects, The Great Learning &amp; The Doctrine of the Mean by Confucius" rel="nofollow noopener">Confucius's Analects etc.</a>).<br /><br />Overall this collection of disparate mystical writings by long-dead Hindus is the early frontrunner for my Most Philosophically Stimulating Sacred Text award. I almost wrote <i>Theo</i>sophically, but their ideas exercised my love of wisdom more than they conveyed to me any wisdom about god. (Screw you, too, Blavatsky! i know you're listening.)<br /><br />I disagreed with most of the metaphysical claims. I quibbled frequently. I pooh-poohed and tsk-tsked ad nauseam. I made snotty gibes. I might've even told the book to fuck off a couple times. Especially by the end when the nagging winds of repetition overwhelmed my ever-threadbare patience.<br /><br />But some of the ideas conveyed had <i>never</i> occurred to me and probably never would have if them old nutters hadn't written them down lo those ~2 millenia ago. Some readers might conclude from such an experience, "It was as if they were written explicitly with me in mind," but iMeMy don't succumb to that particular brand of solipsism: iMeMy will stick with, "This is some deep shit, yo."<br /><br />(Skip to the end of this llllloooooonnnnnng review if you'd like to read some quotes from the experts; they'll give you a better sense of what's waiting for you inside this book.)<br /><br /><b>Translation suggestion</b> Read several side-by-side if you really want to get a decent sense of what the original text might've meant. These works were originally secrets for initiates (and their sons) only that were shared only in private conversations for hundreds of years before finally being written down more than 2000 years ago. Do you s'pose the odds are good that anybody nowadays can translate them with a great degree of precision? Alternately, you could learn to read Sanskrit. (Note: advice coming from a guy who read one English translation and skimmed bits of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/69658.Swami_Nikhilananda" title="Swami Nikhilananda" rel="nofollow noopener">Nikhilananda</a>'s.) <br /><br />Next up, another Hindu treasure, the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6340453.The_Bhagavad_Gita_According_to_Gandhi" title="The Bhagavad Gita According to Gandhi by Mahatma Gandhi" rel="nofollow noopener">Bhagavad Gita</a>, as translated by and commented upon by none other than <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5810891.Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi" rel="nofollow noopener">Mohandas K. Gandhi</a> (i believe the K stands for Kowtipper).<br /><br /><b>If you have bothered to read this far and have an opinion on this matter, please help me.</b> After the Hinduism, Buddhism is my next stop on the world tour of holy shit. I think i oughta read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/777459.The_Long_Discourses_of_the_Buddha_A_Translation_of_the_Digha_Nikaya" title="The Long Discourses of the Buddha A Translation of the Digha Nikaya by Maurice Walshe" rel="nofollow noopener">Digha Nikaya (Buddha's long-winded stuff)</a> first cuz allegedly it's the sacred text most capable of catalyzing a conversion ... and i'm up for a challenge after the Ups. Or i could go for short and simple: Kindle CheapAss's™ edition of Max Müller's translation of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8158952.Dhammapada__canonical_books_of_the_Buddhists" title="Dhammapada, canonical books of the Buddhists by Friedrich Max Müller" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dhammapada (Buddha's sayings)</a> (you'll prolly wanna check out <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/159964.The_Dhammapada" title="The Dhammapada by Gautama Buddha" rel="nofollow noopener">this edition</a> instead). Or, behind door number three, Big B's life story, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/301928.The_Way_of_the_Bodhisattva_A_Translation_of_the_Bodhicharyavatara" title="The Way of the Bodhisattva A Translation of the Bodhicharyavatara by Śāntideva" rel="nofollow noopener">Bodhicharyavatara (the life of the Bodhisattva)</a>. <br /><br /><b>By the way: the goal of the holy shit world book tour</b> To learn something about many of the world's Great Religions simply by reading their sacred texts (i ain't a-goin ta no church and i ain't personally interviewin no devout believers!). My biggest non-philosophical takeaway from the Upanishads is that i can't learn much of anything about the <i>practice</i> of Hinduism from it. Critical texts drive this point home to a certainty and convince me that all sacred texts might have the same relationship to their faiths <i>as practiced by</i> their devotees. We humans declare something to be holy writ, and then there's what we humans do with it, including but not limited to literally and figuratively wiping our asses.<br /><br /><b>Allow iMeMy to dig up some o' the deep shit i mentioned earlier</b> (i.e., i'm gonna babble and babble and babble so you'd be wise to go read something else now; e.g., it's a good time to scroll down to the experts' quotes)<br /><br /><u>the 3 (or 4, or 7?!) levels of consciousness</u> So, i've only ever thought of the Conscious and the Subconscious. I'm limited that way. Like i'm kinda unable to think of more than zero gods. Anyway. The Hindu gurus pointed out for me that there's awake, asleep-and-dreaming, and asleep-but-not-dreaming. This last one ... it's a tricksy one.<br /><br /><u>where does the Self go during dreamless sleep?</u> Good bleeping question. I'm going along, existing nice and steady and aware and all, then i choose to sleep, and then at some point unknown to me and completely irrelevant to my wishes, i just don't exist anymore. And it's as if <i>nothing</i> exists anymore, not me, not you, not any thing at all. (By the way, iMeMy frequently succumb to this kind of solipsism.) <br />I've heard/read arguments that fearing death because it might mean the utter annihilation of the Self/soul is as silly as fearing sleep because during sleep one always completely loses consciousness for at least a little while ... and nobody fears that, right? Uh, perhaps, but maybe that's what little kids fight so strenuously when they're too young to be capable of expressing such thoughts?<br /><br /><u>how does the Self come back from dreamless sleep?</u> Another good bleepin question. It just happens. We don't make it happen. And if you tell me that it's simple brain chemistry, then are you tellin me that i'm actually just a biomachine? (I hope not, cuz them's fightin words.) I mean, i know there are all kinds of things that my body does without me willing them, but this is my consciousness and lack thereof we're talkin about. Even though "Absence of proof does not mean proof of absence," i can't think of any other consciousness-related actions that happen unwittingly. <br />And what about creatures to whom we attribute lower consciousnesses? Do bears lose consciousness during long periods of hibernation? They can't be actively dreaming the whole time. Is it harder to bring the bear Self back from that long sleep? And what about worms? Thanks to last night's torrential rains, this morning i saw my first sidewalk-languishing worms of the neoSpringtime. Assuming they're underground all Winter and assuming they're not dancing around down there in the frozen earth, what happens to their already limited consciousnesses during the longdarkfreezetime? [i'm sure you've guessed it by now, but none of these exact discussions is literally covered in the Upanishads; these details are merely idiosyncratic tangents]<br /><br /><u>the Self (atman) and the Über-Self (Brahman)</u> Everybody has an iMeMy that's most uniquely iMeMy to them and that iMeMy seems to be separate from every other most-uniquely-Him/Her; that uniqueness is the Hindu concept <i>atman</i> (fairly translated as "soul"). And then there's Brahman. Brahman is to the Universe what atman is to each of you. So, the Universe has a soul. Except Brahman actually is the Universe. Including you. Including iMeMy. Including our "individual" atmans. And everything yearns to become part of Brahman. The yearning one feels, that's awareness separateness and awareness of the inadequacy of separateness. Unity is what everyone seeks. <br />I'm not totally in love with this idea, but it does get me thinking. And i definitely do not dig the orthodox Hindu view that the absolute absorption of the individual Self into Brahman is the ultimate bliss ... iMeMy wanna hang on to my Self, pretty please and thank you very much Mr Brahman sir ... that's the kind of solipsist i am.<br /><br /><u>Consciousness Is</u> The Universe, that is. Consciousness. That's where everything came from. THAT is the Big Bang. There was no consciousness / There was no-consciousness. And then ... Is. <br />Like the transition to/from asleep-not-dreaming. <br />It blows my mind. I promise i'm not high. Maybe that would make it even mindblowier; i really wouldn't know, but iMeMy can't help but consider the possibilities of hallucinogenics and psychotropics.<br />I've always wondered what makes me conscious and what the hell is consciousness? How can anything be conscious and how is it different from something like a rock which i presume has no consciousness?<br /><br /><u>that brings me to the infinite line metaphor</u> My first idea for a personal attempt to trap Brahman within a finite image was the universal sea of seltzer water metaphor, but i've given up on that one. <br />I hope you know of Etch A Sketch™ or even <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/98573.Harold_and_the_Purple_Crayon" title="Harold and the Purple Crayon by Crockett Johnson" rel="nofollow noopener">Harold and the Purple Crayon</a>. <br />Start by picturing Harold's simple drawings or the simple things that most of us are capable of drawing with an Etch A Sketch. <br />Now imagine you have your favorite drawing implement &amp; medium. Imagine a simple line drawing of a house, like what you would've done at 5 years old. <br />Now, draw that picture in one continuous line. And now do it in one continuous line that never crosses itself. And now the same restraints plus it must have 3-dimensional perspective. <br />Can you imagine being able to draw a truly 3-dimensional house this way? How about a 3-dimensional house that persists in time for 5 minutes and accounts for (i.e., displays) all movement inside and outside? <br />Now add the following inward and outward quantum leaps: include everything everywhere always, even thoughts and feelings and possibilities and ....<br /><i>That</i> one continuous never-doubling back, never-intersecting line is the Oneness of the interconnectedness of everything. Delving deeper into matter only reveals the hyperintricacy of Brahman's line-drawing. <br />And that beautifully simple English preposition works in both directions: the line-drawing is "of Brahman" in the sense that Brahman creates it (by being), but it's also "of Brahman" in that the line-drawing depicts Brahman because Brahman is that which Is. <br />The guru has just walked up to me to say for the umpteenth time, "<i>Neti, neti</i> [not that, not that]," so i shall finally stop trying to explain (capture) the infinite.<br /><br />Come to think of it, you don't gotta read the Upanishads, you only gotta read what i have wroten here, you lazy luckouts. The Ups prolly ain't gonna do it for you. Especially if the stuff i already writed gets you excited. <br /><br />I don't know, whatever, read the Ups or don't read the Ups. Read pieces parts. Read about pieces parts all. I thought it was pretty cool stuff in the abstract, but ultimately i wouldn't dedicate 12 years of my life to studying under a guru in the hopes of holding the winner in this particular universal lottery.<br /><br />As usual, i've told you nothing (very little?) about the book that i am ostensibly "reviewing." <br /><br /><b>What some truly smart people have said about the Ups</b><br />From <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/54296.Eknath_Easwaran" title="Eknath Easwaran" rel="nofollow noopener">Eknath Easwaran</a>'s intro:<blockquote>[They] are not philosophy. They do not explain or develop a line of argument. They are ... "something seen," and the student to whom they were taught was expected not only to listen to the words but to ... make their truths an integral part of character, conduct, and consciousness. (22-23)<br />[Only] a few ever hear these truths; of those who hear, only a few understand, and of those only a handful attain the goal. (24)<br />[The] sages of the Upanishads wanted more than explanations of the outside world. They sought principles that would unify and explain the world within the mind. If the observer observes through the medium of consiousness and the world too is observed in consciousness, should not the same laws apply to both? (28; clearly this point is debatable, but...)<br />What do they [the Ups] report? They tell us, first, that whatever we are, whatever we may have done, there is in each of us an inalienable Self that is divine.... [Love] is the first and last commandment of this realization, for the same Self dwells in all.... They call us to the discovery of a realm deep within ourselves which is our native state.... They place us at home in a compassionate universe, where nothing is "other" than ourselves--and they urge us to treat that universe with reverence, for there is nothing in the world but God.... Last, most significantly, the Upanishads tell us that our native state is a realm where death cannot reach, which can be attained here in this life by those willing to devote their lives to the necessary purification of consciousness.... In this compassionate view life becomes a kind of school in which the individual self is constantly evolving, growing life after life toward a fully human stature. The goal is realization of one's true nature: not matter, embodied or disembodied, but the uncreated Self. (42-45)</blockquote>From <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Michael%20M%20Nagler" title="Michael M Nagler" rel="nofollow noopener">Michael M Nagler</a>'s afterword:<blockquote>First, the Upanishads offer a noble, exalted vision of human nature.... Second, while [they] are wrapped in a good bit of mythology and ritual, that wrapping comes off pretty easily. What we are left with is pure mysticism: a penetrating and remarkably comprehensive vision of Truth.... Third, [they] are scientific [errrm...] and experiential [OK, sure]. They don't say, "Believe this"; they say, "This we have seen...". More: their outlook is nonjudgmental. (295-296)<br />Tradition has isolated four powerful formulaic utterances (<i>mahāvākyas</i>) embedded in the early Upanishads. One is <i>sarvam idam brahma,</i> "All is Brahman".... (316)<br />The others are <i>ayam ātmā brahma,</i> "The Self is Brahman," <i>prajnām brahma,</i> "Consciousness is Brahman," and <i>aham brahmāsmi,</i> "I am Brahman." (335; note re: quote from 316)<br />Not surprisingly, "You are That" (<i>tat tvam asi</i>) is one of the mahāvākyas (316; which apparently makes <i>five</i> mahāvākyas?)</blockquote>From <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/168263.Paul_Deussen" title="Paul Deussen" rel="nofollow noopener">Paul Deussen</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9651189.The_Philosophy_of_the_Upanishads" title="The Philosophy of the Upanishads by Paul Deussen" rel="nofollow noopener">The Philosophy of the Upanishads</a>:<blockquote>[The] leading ideas of the Upanishads [are] the doctrine, namely, of the sole reality of the Atman, of its evolution as the universe, its identity with the soul... (8)<br />This identity of the Brahman and the atman, of God and the soul, is the fundamental thought of the entire doctrine of the Upanishads. (39)<br />The necessary premises of all religion are, as <a href="http://plato.stanford.edu/entries/kant/" rel="nofollow noopener">Kant</a> frequently expounds: (1) The existence of God, (2) the immortality of the soul, (3) the freedom of the will (without which no morality is possible). These three essential conditions of man's salvation—God, immortality, and freedom—are conceivable only if the universe is mere appearance and not reality ([to use the Hindu word for it] mere <i>maya</i> and not the atman), and they break down irretrievably should this empirical reality, wherein we live, be found to constitute the true essence of things. (45)<br />The teaching of Yajnavalkhya [a prominent sage depicted within the Ups] ... is a daring, uncompromising, eccentric idealism (comparable to that of <a href="http://plato.stanford.edu/entries/parmenides/" rel="nofollow noopener">Parmenides</a>), which is summed up in three propositions: ... (1) The atman is the knowing subject within us.... (2) The atman, as the knowing subject, is itself unknowable.... (3) The atman is the sole reality. (399-400)</blockquote><b>Why <i>iMeMy</i> am not a Hindu</b> As stated earlier, i just can't give up my Self like i'm supposed to. I love my Self too much to let it go EVEN if that means guaranteed blissful (but selfless) unity with The Ultimate Oneness of Everything (my term). (cf. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12994859.Why_I_Am_Not_a_Hindu" title="Why I Am Not a Hindu by Dr Ramendra" rel="nofollow noopener">Why I Am Not a Hindu</a> whose author has much more quotidian quibbles agin this religion.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="6610928134354109279" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_503838728" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/503838728" />
    <a title="Philip Cartwright" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15102137-philip-cartwright"><img alt="Philip Cartwright" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1356889595p2/15102137.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/503838728?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Philip Cartwright" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Philip Cartwright" href="/user/show/15102137-philip-cartwright">Philip Cartwright</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780199540259" href="/book/show/4595558-upanisads">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15102137-philip-cartwright?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15102137-philip-cartwright?shelf=religion">religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer503838728" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14299719669535302997">A fascinating, sometimes haunting, sometimes baffling read. Of course, these sacred scriptures don't give a full picture of Hinduism by themselves - for that I'd need greater familiarity with the numerous ceremonies, chants, rituals and stories that go with them. Nevertheless, the Upanishads provide a mesmerising window onto what is (for me) a very foreign world.<br/><br/>I hesitate to attempt much analysis of such a venerable text - especially after just a first read-through. But I will offer this: on on</span>
  <span id="freeText14299719669535302997" style="display:none">A fascinating, sometimes haunting, sometimes baffling read. Of course, these sacred scriptures don't give a full picture of Hinduism by themselves - for that I'd need greater familiarity with the numerous ceremonies, chants, rituals and stories that go with them. Nevertheless, the Upanishads provide a mesmerising window onto what is (for me) a very foreign world.<br /><br />I hesitate to attempt much analysis of such a venerable text - especially after just a first read-through. But I will offer this: on one level the Upanishads provide a means of connecting mankind to the world (and by "the world" I mean not just the earth and its objects, but celestial objects, gods and spirits). It spells out a web of intricate symbolism whereby everything stands for something else and everything is connected to everything else. Everything grasps and is grasped. But on another level, the symbolism is a means of <i>transcending</i> the world. Here, of course, I'm thinking of Brahman: the great unknowable that stands at the heart of all existence and can only be defined by a series of negations: "Not__ Not__ Not__". <br /><br />As the Upanishads progress this notion of Brahman increasingly takes centre-stage. Consequently I found the mystical message became increasingly familiar: the perceived world is an illusion, wealth is a trap, etc, etc. I'm not criticising this, but I did find the earlier books (the Brhadaranyaka and Chandogya Upanishads) intriguing precisely because they are more ambiguous or elusive on the issue. <br /><br />The Oxford World's Classics edition has a helpful introduction and detailed end-notes.<br /><br />Finally, one of the many verses that caught my eye: <br /><br />I do not think<br /> that I know it well;<br />But I know not<br /> that I do not know.<br />Who of us knows that,<br /> he does know that;<br />But he knows not,<br /> that he does not know.<br /><br />It's envisioned by one who envisions it not;<br /> but one who envisions it knows it not.<br />And those who perceive it perceive it not;<br /> but it's perceived by those who perceive it not.<br /><br />Kena Upanishad, Chapter 2</span>
  <a data-text-id="14299719669535302997" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1460674903" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1460674903" />
    <a title="Julian Meynell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12278245-julian-meynell"><img alt="Julian Meynell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1346095447p2/12278245.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1460674903?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julian Meynell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julian Meynell" href="/user/show/12278245-julian-meynell">Julian Meynell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12278245-julian-meynell?shelf=ancient">ancient</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12278245-julian-meynell?shelf=indian">indian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12278245-julian-meynell?shelf=religion-source">religion-source</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12278245-julian-meynell?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1460674903" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11582166965469141161">I read the version translated by Juan Mascaro and I want to talk about his treatment before moving onto the work. I found Mascaro's introduction to be a bit flaky. Mascaro is fundamentally a mystic who tries to see all religions and most great figures in literature and philosophy as reflecting the same one mystical reality. While of course there is some merit in that view, it hides more than it reveals. In the translation, this gave me some concerns where Mascaro is using Christian language and </span>
  <span id="freeText11582166965469141161" style="display:none">I read the version translated by Juan Mascaro and I want to talk about his treatment before moving onto the work. I found Mascaro's introduction to be a bit flaky. Mascaro is fundamentally a mystic who tries to see all religions and most great figures in literature and philosophy as reflecting the same one mystical reality. While of course there is some merit in that view, it hides more than it reveals. In the translation, this gave me some concerns where Mascaro is using Christian language and I was not sure if it was accurate translation or a reflection of Mascaro's philosophical agenda. Also, apparently the entirety of the Upanishads is about equal in size to the Bible and its hard to tell then how reflective this sample is of the whole. Having said all that, Mascaro's translation is good and has a lot of literary power in its plain language use. It seemed to me a well worth reading selection, but I was left with the intent to read the whole of the Upanishad's at some point in the future.<br /><br />The Upanishad's themselves are good. The selections in this book are very much the core religious doctrines, but their expression is beautiful and they were a pleasure to read. In many ways I preferred them to the Bhagavad Gita which is really my only relevant point of comparison. I do think that they are more interesting and insightful than most other religious texts, although I am at heart a Spinozist and I was often struck by how close they were to the doctrines of Spinoza. Probably every person who was to be literate and understand the major ideas of the world religions should read them. They are also very pretty. However, the Bible could be made to look a lot more pretty than it really is, if a selection this small was made of it.<br /><br />Anyway, you should probably read this book, and I should probably read the whole thing at some point in the future.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11582166965469141161" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText305904597452464992" style="display:none">"Om. Poornamatha Poornamitham Poornatpoornamudachyathe<br />Poornasya Poornamathaya Poornamevavasishyathe<br />Om Shaanthi! Shaanthi! The infinite proceeds from the infinite.<br />(Then) taking the infinitude of the infinite (universe), it remains as the infinite (Brahman) alone."<br /><br />The author's translation:-<br />"All this is full, all that is full.<br />From fullness, fullness comes.<br />When fullness is taken from fullness,<br />Fullness still remains."<br /><br />Manthra is to be chanted as it is. <tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review697240773">
  <td colspan="2">

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_99944'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/99944.The_Bhagavad_Gita"><img alt="The Bhagavad Gita" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1383059639l/99944._SX98_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('KyF1F7/+zmjrOG0w+ipoLpqqC5PLwGfEpNFIkrvCtahnHdzDbbBmNVJ1kjwsA/y6Y7bJiX5YIe8pAZyNcYuEyA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('iA6DcHEiwjyZdCpd06eTDwG/QcAPTcoz53A7lteqD+nEMiqko2xqYSA51VEFjgeb+KOD2rrVjBhqoO+JHeM+iQ==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Se cree que sus orígenes se remontan al siglo IV a.C. ya que forma parte de los smíriti hindúes (textos no aportados directamente por Dios, sino transmitidos por la tradición). El Ramayana es una de las obras literarias más importantes de la India, este libro estableció algunas pautas fundamentales dentro de la poesía sánscrita y ha tenido una gran influencia en el arte y la cultura del sureste de Asia, El texto de esta obra es alegórico definitivamente, cuenta la historia del semidiós Rama, quien representa todo lo bueno, y su encantada lucha contra los asuras, que ostentan el poder del mal. En la mitología hindú, asura es el símbolo equivalente al titán o demonio gigante, así que en esta epopeya tenemos el mito de la lucha entre semidioses y demonios, es decir, el combate entre los poderes de la luz y las fuerzas malignas de la oscuridad.\n\nRama constituye el ideal del hombre sabio, fuerte, justo y bueno, en él se funden todas las cualidades del gobernante y del guerrero. The King of Death tests his resolve, but the teenager stands firm, demanding answers to the age-old questions, &quot;What is the purpose of life? What happens to me when I die?&quot; Death emerges as the perfect spiritual guide — direct, uncompromising, and challenging. Easwaran’s approach to the Katha is both practical and universal. With engaging candor, eloquence, and wit, Paramahansa Yogananda narrates the inspiring chronicle of his life: the experiences of his remarkable childhood, encounters with many saints and sages during his youthful search throughout India for an illumined teacher, ten years of training in the hermitage of a revered yoga master, and the thirty years that he lived and taught in America. Also recorded here are his meetings with Mahatma Gandhi, Rabindranath Tagore, Luther Burbank, the Catholic stigmatist Therese Neumann, and other celebrated spiritual personalities of East and West. \n\nAutobiography of a Yogi is at once a beautifully written account of an exceptional life and a profound introduction to the ancient science of Yoga and its time-honored tradition of meditation. The author clearly explains the subtle but definite laws behind both the ordinary events of everyday life and the extraordinary events commonly termed miracles. His absorbing life story thus becomes the background for a penetrating and unforgettable look at the ultimate mysteries of human existence. \n\nConsidered a modern spiritual classic, the book has been translated into more than twenty languages and is widely used as a text and reference work in colleges and universities. A perennial bestseller since it was first published sixty years ago, Autobiography of a Yogi has found its way into the hearts of millions of readers around the world.<\/span>\n  <a       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_560946'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/560946.The_Yoga_Sutras_of_Patanjali?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Yoga Sutras of Patanjali<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/80565.Pata_jali?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Patañjali<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.27 avg rating &mdash; 2,039 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 400\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer9748406952348251933\">“A wonderful translation, full of contemporary insight yet luminous with eternal truth.”—Jacob NeedlemanThe Yoga Sutras were cast in their present form in India around the third century b.c. Yoga is from the Sanskrit root meaning “union,” and a sutra<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText9748406952348251933\" style=\"display:none\">“A wonderful translation, full of contemporary insight yet luminous with eternal truth.”—Jacob NeedlemanThe Yoga Sutras were cast in their present form in India around the third century b.c. Yoga is from the Sanskrit root meaning “union,” and a sutra is a thread or aphoristic verse. The basic questions “Who am I?” “Where am I going?” “What is the purpose of life?” are asked by each new generation, and Patanjali’s answers form one of the oldest and most vibrant spiritual texts in the world. He explains what yoga is, how it works, and exactly how to purify the mind and let it settle into absolute stillness. This stillness is our own Self. It is the indispensable ground for Enlightenment, which is the ultimate goal of all our aspirations.Alistair Shearer’s lucid introduction and superb translation, fully preserving Patanjali’s jewel-like style, bring these ancient but vital teachings to those who seek the path of self-knowledge today.Bell Tower’s series, Sacred Teachings, offers essential spiritual classics from all traditions. Still an integral part of India\'s cultural and religious expression, the Ramayana was originally composed by the Sanskrit poet Valmiki around 300 b.c. The epic of Prince Rama\'s betrayal, exile, and struggle to rescue his faithful wife, Sita, from the clutches of a demon and to reclaim his throne has profoundly affected the literature, art, and culture of South and Southeast Asia-an influence most likely unparalleled in the history of world literature, except, possibly, for the Bible. Throughout the centuries, countless versions of the epic have been produced in numerous formats and languages. But previous English versions have been either too short to capture the magnitude of the original; too secular in presenting what is, in effect, scripture; or dry, line-by-line translations. Now novelist Ramesh Menon has rendered the tale in lyrical prose that conveys all the beauty and excitement of the original, while making this spiritual and literary classic accessible to a new An Indian yogi named Svatmarama wrote the Hatha Yoga Pradipika in the fifteenth century C.E. Drawing on his own experience and older works now lost, he wrote this book for the student of Yoga. He wrote this book for you.\n\nSample Verses:\nYoga succeeds by these six: enthusiasm, openness, courage, knowledge of the truth, determination, and solitude.\n\nSuccess is achieved neither by wearing the right clothes nor by talking about it. Practice alone brings success. This is the truth, without a doubt.\n\nWhen the breath is unsteady, the mind is unsteady. When the breath is steady, the mind is steady, and the yogi becomes steady. Therefore one should restrain the breath.\n\nAs salt and water become one when mixed, so the unity of self and mind is called samadhi.\n\nHe who binds the breath, binds the mind. He who binds the mind, binds the breath.\n\nCenter the self in space and space in the self. Make everything space, then don’t think of anything.\n\nEmpty within, empty without, empty like a pot in space. Full within, full without, full like a pot in the ocean.\n\nDon’t think of external things and don’t think of internal things. Abandon all thoughts, then don’t think of anything.\n\nReviews:\n“Beautifully printed and translated. <span id="freeTextContainer7881018252301882627">Books can be attributed to "Anonymous" for several reasons: <br/><br/>* They are officially published under that name <br/>* They are traditional stories not attributed to a specific author <br/>* They are religious texts not generally attributed to a specific author <br/><br/>Books whose authorship is merely uncertain should be attributed to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4699102.Unknown' title='Unknown' rel='nofollow noopener'>Unknown</a>.</span>

last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
